class="article__body-croppable"><div class="block-content"><div class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><span>Earth last year shattered global annual </span><a href="http://9news.com.au/heat" title="heat" target="" rel=""><span>heat</span></a><span> records, flirted with the world's agreed-upon warming threshold and showed more signs of a feverish planet, the </span><a href="http://9news.com.au/europe" title="European" target="" rel=""><span>European</span></a><span> climate agency has declared.</span></div></div><div class="block-content"><div class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><span>In one of the first of several teams of </span><a href="http://9news.com.au/science" title="science" target="" rel=""><span>science</span></a><span> agencies to calculate how off-the-charts warm 2023 was, the European climate agency Copernicus said the year was 1.48 degrees above pre-industrial times. </span></div></div><div class="block-content"><div class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><span>That's barely below the 1.5 degrees limit the world hoped to stay within in the 2015 Paris climate accord to avoid the most </span><a href="http://9news.com.au/climate-change" title="severe effects of warming" target="" rel=""><span>severe effects of warming</span></a><span>.</span></div></div><div><div id="adspot-mobile-medium"></div></div><div class="block-content"><div class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><strong><span>READ MORE: </span></strong><a href="https://www.9news.com.au/world/nanoplastics-bottled-water-contains-thousands-so-small-they-can-invade-the-bodys-cells-study-says/2c735bb5-9b31-457c-9bd7-0606292b9c54"><strong><span>Bottled water contains thousands of nanoplastics so small they can invade the body's cells, study says</span></strong></a></div></div><figure class="article__image"><img Photo/Emilio Morenatti, File)</figcaption></figure><div class="block-content"><div class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><span>January 2024 is on track to be so warm that for the first time a 12-month period will exceed the 1.5-degree threshold, Copernicus Deputy Director Samantha Burgess said today.</span></div></div><div class="block-content"><div class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><span>Scientists have repeatedly said Earth would need to average 1.5 degrees of warming over two or three decades to be a technical breach of the threshold.</span></div></div><div class="block-content"><div class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><span>The 1.5-degree goal "has to be (kept) alive because lives are at risk and choices have to be made", Burgess said. </span></div></div><div><div class="OUTBRAIN" data-src="//www.9news.com.au/world/climate-change-update-earth-shattered-global-heat-record-in-2023-european-climate-agency-copernicus/2c866637-2d78-4410-9b33-7f2a0d0e2ec7" data-widget-id="AR_5" data-reactroot=""></div></div><div class="block-content"><div class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><span>"And these choices don't impact you and I but they impact our children and our grandchildren."</span></div></div><div class="block-content"><div class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><span>The record heat made life miserable and sometimes deadly in Europe, North America, China and many other places last year. </span></div></div><div class="block-content"><div class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><span>But scientists say a warming climate is also to blame for more extreme weather events, like the lengthy drought that devastated the Horn of Africa, the torrential downpours that wiped out dams and killed thousands in Libya and the Canada wildfires that goULFa"><span>For the first time, nations meeting for annual United Nations climate talks in December </span><a href="https://www.9news.com.au/world/cop28-update-dubai-climate-summit-delegates-agree-transition-away-fossil-fuels/0bea9f0e-df93-4262-9911-9aabf2af260f" title="agreed that the world needs to transition away from the fossil fuels" target="" rel=""><span>agreed that the world needs to transition away from the fossil fuels</span></a><span> that are causing climate change, but they set no concrete requirements to do so.</span></div></div><div class="block-content"><div class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><span>Copernicus calculated that the global average temperature for 2023 was about one-sixth of a degree warmer than the old record set in 2016. While that seems a small amount in global record-keeping, it's an exceptionally large margin for the new record, Burgess said. Earth's average temperature for 2023 was 14.98, Copernicus calculated.</span></div></div><div class="block-content"><div class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><span>"It was record-breaking for seven months. We had the warmest June, July, August, September, October, November, December," Burgess said. </span></div></div><div class="block-content"><div class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><span>"It wasn't just a season or a month that was exceptional. It was exceptional for over half the year."</span></div></div><div class="block-content"><div class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><strong><span>READ MORE: </span></strong><a class="block-content"><div class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><span>There are several factors that made 2023 the warmest year on record, but by far the biggest factor was the ever-increasing amount of greenhouse gases in the atmosphere that trap heat, Burgess said. Those gases come from the burning of coal, oil and natural gas.</span></div></div><div class="block-content"><div class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><span>Other factors included the natural El Nino — a temporary warming of the central Pacific that alters weather worldwide — other natural oscillations in the Arctic, southern and Indian oceans, increased solar activity and the 2022 eruption of an undersea volcano that sent water vapour into the atmosphere, Burgess said.</span></div></div><div class="block-content"><div class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><span>Malte Meinshausen, a University of Melbourne climate scientist, said about 1.3 degrees of the warming comes from greenhouse gases, with another 0.1 degrees from El Nino and the rest being smaller causes.</span></div></div><div class="block-content"><div class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><span>Given El Nino and record ocean heat levels, Burgess said it's "extremely likely" that 2024 will be even hotter than 2023.</span></div></div><figure class="article__image"><img class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><span>Copernicus records only go back to 1940 and are based on a combination of observations and forecast models. Other groups, including the United States' National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration and NASA, the United Kingdom's Meteorological Office and Berkeley Earth go back to the mid-1800s and will announce their calculations for 2023 on Friday, with expectations of record-breaking marks.</span></div></div><div class="block-content"><div class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><span>The Japanese Meteorological Agency, which uses similar techniques as Copernicus and goes back to 1948, late last month estimated that it was the warmest year at 1.47 degrees above pre-industrial levels. </span></div></div><div class="block-content"><div class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><span>The University of Alabama Huntsville global dataset, which uses satellite measurements rather than ground data and dates to 1979, last week also found it the hottest year on record, but not by as much.</span></div></div><div class="block-content"><div class="styles__Container-sc-1ylecsg-0 goULFa"><span>Though actual observations only date back less than two centuries, several scientists say evidence from tree rings and ice cores suggest this is the warmest the Earth has been in more than 100,000 years.</span></div></div><figure class="article__image"><img "}],[{"type":"#text","value":"\"Humans were around before that but it's certainly fair to say it's the hottest since humans became civilised, depending on the definition of 'civilised'.\""}],[{"type":"#text","value":"Amid record hot months were days that were downright unprecedented hot across the globe."}],[{"type":"#text","value":"For the first time, Copernicus recorded a day where the world averaged at least 2 degrees more than pre-industrial times. It happened twice and narrowly missed a third day around Christmas, Burgess said."}],[{"type":"#text","value":"And for the first time, every day of the year was at least one degree warmer than pre-industrial times. For nearly half the year — 173 days — the world was 1.5 degrees warmer than the mid-1800s."}],[{"type":"#text","value":"Meinshausen, the Australian climate scientist, said it's natural for the public to wonder whether the 1.5-degree target is lost.
